    Discovery of a Potent and Orally Bioavailable Cyclophilin Inhibitor Derived from the Sanglifehrin Macrocycle

    Abstract

    Cyclophilins are a family of peptidyl-prolyl isomerases that are implicated in a wide range of diseases including hepatitis C. Our aim was to discover through total synthesis an orally bioavailable, non-immunosuppressive cyclophilin (Cyp) inhibitor with potent anti-hepatitis C virus (HCV) activity that could serve as part of an all oral antiviral combination therapy. An initial lead 2 derived from the sanglifehrin A macrocycle was optimized using structure based design to produce a potent and orally bioavailable inhibitor 3. The macrocycle ring size was reduced by one atom, and an internal hydrogen bond drove improved permeability and drug-like properties. 3 demonstrates potent Cyp inhibition (Kd = 5 nM), potent anti-HCV 2a activity (EC50 = 98 nM), and high oral bioavailability in rat (100%) and dog (55%). The synthetic accessibility and properties of 3 support its potential as an anti-HCV agent and for interrogating the role of Cyp inhibition in a variety of diseases.

    Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Cyclophilins are a family of peptidyl-prolyl isomerases that are involved in a variety of biological processes.(1,2) They are known to be mediators of the immunosuppressive effects of cyclosporin A (CsA), which acts by simultaneously binding to calcineurin and the cyclophilin (Cyp) isomerase active site.(3,4) The modified CsA analogue, NIM-811, is an inhibitor of Cyp isomerase activity but does not bind calcineurin, making it a valuable tool for studying the non-immunosuppressive biology of Cyp isomerases.(5,6) These studies have implicated Cypâs in a variety of diseases ranging from asthma to viral replication.(7â10) Indeed, several non-immunosuppressive cyclosporin analogues discovered after NIM-811, e.g., alisporivir and SCY-635, have been investigated in clinical trials as potential therapies for hepatitis C virus (HCV) infection.(11,12) Alisporivir, the furthest advanced, demonstrated an antiviral effect in HCV-infected patients, but was halted following toxicity observations when dosed in combination with ribavirin and pegylated interferon.(13) The HCV NS5a protein was identified as the binding target of intracellular Cyp A, one member of the family of human cyclophilins, and although the exact mechanism of viral inhibition remains to be elucidated, it is presumed that interference of the Cyp A and HCV NS5a protein interaction prevents viral replication.(14â18) Cyp inhibitors have also been demonstrated to reduce hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection through interaction with the hepatocyte sodium taurocholate cotransporting polypeptide (NTCP) surface receptor that is required for entry of HBV particles into hepatocytes.(19,20)
    Despite the broad therapeutic potential of Cyp inhibition, progress in the field has been hampered by the fact that potent Cyp inhibitors are derived from structurally complex natural products such as CsA and sanglifehrins.(21â26) While semisynthesis strategies have been successfully utilized to improve upon these natural products, this approach fundamentally limits the structureâactivity relationship (SAR) investigations necessary for improving oral pharmacokinetic (PK) properties. Synthetic small molecule inhibitors have been identified from screening campaigns, but, to the best of our knowledge, no highly potent, small molecule with oral properties has been reported to date.(27,28)
    Our interest in Cyp inhibition stemmed from the potential to administer an oral Cyp A inhibitor in combination with other oral anti-HCV agents as part of a pan genotype, all oral, anti-HCV regimen. It was anticipated that inhibition of a host enzyme such as Cyp A would confer a high barrier to resistance and broad, pan-genotypic activity. We therefore initiated an effort to discover an orally bioavailable, small molecule Cyp A inhibitor that would have a low propensity for drugâdrug interactions. Our first report(29) detailed efforts based on the sanglifehrin A macrocycle, following encouraging reports of anti-HCV activity of a sanglifehrin derived semisynthetic analogue AAE931.(30) This work culminated in the identification of leads 1 and 2 (Figure 1) that had potent Cyp inhibition and modest ant-HCV activity but poor pharmacokinetics. Here we describe the structure-guided optimization of these leads into a potent and orally bioavailable Cyp A inhibitor 3 (Figure 1). A pivotal part of our strategy was to maintain a tractable convergent synthesis that employed readily accessible building blocks. The key advances from 2 to 3 are described in three phases, beginning with the isoquinoline replacement of the styrene in 2 and a reduction in macrocycle ring size to a 21-membered ring. These modifications improved binding affinity and anti-HCV replicon potency in addition to reducing human plasma protein binding. In the second phase, the C1 lactone and C13 lactam groups were switched along with changing from isoquinoline to quinoline, which introduced an intramolecular hydrogen bond. This resulted in significantly improved membrane permeability leading to high oral absorption. The final phase included modification of the C14 substituents to a spiro-1,3-dioxane that lowered log D, reduced oxidative metabolism, and also reduced pregnane X receptor (PXR) activation, which was a liability with respect to drugâdrug interactions. Compound 3 has high binding affinity, a tractable synthesis, and high oral bioavailability in rat and dog. While 3 is a potential oral development candidate for anti-HCV combination regimens, it also has utility in interrogating the role of Cyp inhibition in a variety of other preclinical disease models.

    Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    A time-resolved fluorescence resonance energy transfer (TR-FRET) biochemical assay assessed binding affinity of the compounds toward Cyp A.(29) The HCV replicon genotype 2a assay in huh-7 cells served as a cell-based functional assay (EC50) of Cyp inhibition. The free fraction (% free) in human plasma was determined using equilibrium dialysis between human plasma and buffer. The ratio of cell culture media (CCM) % free and human plasma % free was used to calculate the human plasma-adjusted (pa) EC50. For example, 2 is 59% free in CCM/buffer and 12% free in human plasma/buffer resulting in a 4.9-fold human plasma/CCM shift and a calculated paEC50 â 1.6 Î¼M. Exposures of 2 exceeding 1.6 Î¼M would therefore be required to maintain Cyp inhibition above 50%, so significant improvements in the paEC50 potency of 2 were desirable. Oral absorption potential was assessed in vitro using log D and Caco-2 assays, while clearance was optimized using predictions from in vitro microsomal stability assays. In vivo correlations to the in vitro PK properties were determined in rat, dog, and cynomolgus monkey following intravenous and oral dosing.

    In the discovery of 2, the binding mode of the macrocycle to Cyp A was elucidated wherein the styrene group forms a Ï-stacking interaction with arginine 55 (Figure 2A).(29) This new interaction enabled the m-tyrosine to be replaced by alanine, thereby reducing molecular weight and hydrogen bond donor count without a loss in potency. Given the importance of the arginine 55 Ï-stacking interaction, our initial efforts to optimize 2 explored a variety of aromatic replacements of styrene, including biphenyl, phenol, and bicyclic heteroaryls.(31â33) This culminated in the identification of isoquinolines 4â9 (Scheme 1).
    Analogues 4â9 were synthesized using methods similar to those reported earlier, employing ring closing metathesis (RCM) in the key macrocyclization step (Scheme 1).(29) The valine terminus of tripeptide 10 was N-BOC deprotected and the amine coupled with unsaturated acid precursors (e.g., 14). The trichloroethyl (TCE) ester was then hydrolyzed and the acid was re-esterified with isoquinoline 17 to generate the required RCM precursors 12aâe. The acid 14 was prepared in three steps from Oppolzerâs sultam 13, using methods similar to those described previously for 2.(29) The chiral (R)-hydroxyalkyl isoquinoline, 17, was prepared in a Stille coupling, acid hydrolysis, and Noyori reduction sequence, that generated 16 stereoselectively, followed by a final Stille vinylation. The RCM was conducted using Hoveyda-Grubbs second generation catalyst at a concentration of 3 mM to provide 4â7 in unoptimized yields ranging from 31 to 63%. The yield was reduced to 23% for the 21-membered ring analogue 8. In example 8, the RCM substrate 12e containing the trisubstituted pent-3-enoic acid was found to be superior to but-3-enoic acid (not shown), which cyclized under similar conditions but provided a mixture of cis and trans double bond isomers in <10% combined yield. Hydrogenation of the unsaturated bond in 8 using Pd/C afforded the reduced analogue 9.
    The binding affinity, replicon activity, and in vitro PK properties for isoquinolines 4â9 are shown in Table 1. Compound 4 exhibited a â¼5-fold improvement in HCV replicon 2a potency versus 2 and established the superiority of the isoquinoline series over styrene. Removal of the C14 and C15 substituents, compound 5, maintained binding affinity but exhibited weaker replicon 2a potency, implying a role for the substitutions in this region for generating optimal cell based activity. Compound 5 is also more polar, demonstrating a lower log D and poorer Caco-2 permeability in the apical to basolateral direction. This property was not altogether surprising since we had observed in our earlier report the structural feature in which the C15 methoxy oxygen in 4 can form an intramolecular hydrogen bond with the C13 NH, thereby reducing its overall polarity.(29) Despite the weaker antiviral potency of 5, it provided a good basis for the rapid exploration of the relationship between macrocycle ring size and Cyp A binding affinity, a relationship that had not been previously reported for the sanglifehrin based inhibitors. Increasing ring size to 23- and 24-membered rings, analogues 6 and 7, respectively, reduced binding affinity relative to 5, but in contrast, the smaller 21-membered ring, 8, improved binding affinity to below that of CsA. Moreover, the smaller ring had a lower log D and reduced human plasma protein binding (Table 1, column 5, 32% vs 12%). Indeed, 8 had the lowest calculated paEC50 = 258 nM of the isoquinoline series and was a 6-fold improvement over 2. Compounds 4â9 showed >100-fold selectivity in the replicon EC50 relative to the MT4 cell line CC50 (Table 1, column 4), a cell line sensitive to the potential cytotoxic effects of compounds. Interestingly, the smaller macrocycle 8 also displayed a significantly improved microsomal predicted clearance (Table 1, column 7), presumably due to its lower log D or, alternatively, the shorter more compact C14âC17 spacer region contributing fewer metabolic soft spots relative to 5. Saturated analogue 9 was significantly weaker in binding affinity suggesting that the conformational rigidity of the macrocycle was a critical feature of potent binding.
    The X-ray structure of isoquinoline 8 complexed to Cyp A was solved and compared to styrene 2 (Figure 2B).(29) The tripeptide backbone that forms the key hydrogen bond interactions with the Cyp protein remains in a comparable pose to that of 2. As expected, the isoquinoline heterocycle is positioned over arginine 55 resulting in a Ï-stacking interaction. The most notable difference was the positioning of the C13âC16 atoms between the isoquinoline and the C13 NH, where the removal of one methylene resulted in these atoms moving away from the protein surface. Armed with the structural information on 8 and its promising profile, efforts were focused on further evaluation of this novel 21-membered macrocycle.
    Oral dosing of 8 in dog and cynomolgus monkey revealed low oral bioavailability, likely due to its low log D and poor Caco-2 permeability. This was confirmed in an oral study in portal vein-cannulated dogs that established only 8% of a 5 mg/kg oral dose was being absorbed (data not shown). We sought to improve the permeability and oral properties of 8 through continued SAR exploration of the isoquinoline heterocycle and modifications to the C13âC16 region where modeling suggested that the C14 methylene would likely tolerate substitution. The immediate goal was to raise log D and improve permeability. Naphthyl and quinoline analogues, 18 and 19, respectively, were targeted, along with the C14 gem-dimethyl analogues 20 and 21 (Table 2). The gem-dimethyl groups avoided the need for introducing chirality into this region, and it was thought they would not only raise log D but also improve permeability by shielding the polarity of the nearby C13 lactam group. We also explored the impact of switching the C1 lactone and C13 lactam groups in the context of the quinoline and isoquinoline heterocycles, analogues 34 and 35, respectively (Table 2). In the case of quinoline 34, this could potentially improve permeability through the introduction of an intramolecular hydrogen bond between the quinoline nitrogen and C1 lactam NH.

    Macrolactamization and macrolactonization approaches to the macrocycles were developed as alternative methods to the RCM.(34,35) This strategy permitted the isoquinoline and related heterocycles to be cross coupled with the different C13âC16 unsaturated acids (Scheme 2), prior to coupling with the tripeptide and final macrocyclization described in Schemes 3 and 4. Dibromo intermediate 22 was converted to a monoketone, stereoselectively reduced to the (R)-hydroxyalkyl intermediate 23 using a Noyori reduction, and then coupled with but-3-enoic acid in a Heck reaction to provide 24. The 2-chloroquinoline 25 was converted to the 2-iodo analogue in situ to promote a more efficient Stille coupling with tri-n-butyl(1-ethoxyvinyl)tin, and then the product was subsequently hydrolyzed to the monoketone. Noyori reduction of the ketone generated the (R)-hydroxyalkyl intermediate 26, which was then coupled with the respective unsaturated ester/acid in a Heck reaction to provide 27 and 28 respectively. The gem dimethyl isoquinoline precursor 29 was formed in a Suzuki cross coupling between 2-chloroisoquinoline 16 and the gem-dimethyl boronate ester 31(31) followed by ester hydrolysis. The macrolactonization at C1 (Scheme 3, path a) began with N-BOC removal in tripeptide 10 followed by HATU mediated coupling of the acid precursors 24, 28, or 29 to afford the intermediates 32aâc. The TCE ester, and hydroxyl protecting group present in 32b, were then removed to reveal the seco-acid intermediates, which were cyclized by slow addition over 6â10 h to 2-methyl-6-nitrobenzoic acid anhydride (Shiina conditions(36)) at a maximal concentration of 3 mmol. The C14 gem-dimethyl analogues 20 and 21 were isolated in â¼50% yield without optimization, and the C14 unsubstituted naphthyl analogue 18 in 18% yield. The alternative macrolactamization at C13 (path b) began with deprotection of the TCE ester in tripeptide 10 followed by EDC mediated coupling with 27 to afford 33. Treatment with acid removed the N-BOC and tert-butyl esters to reveal the seco-acid intermediate that macrocyclized to 19 in 10% yield at 10 mM using HATU. The C1 lactam analogues, quinoline 34 and isoquinoline 35, required the amine precursors described in Scheme 4. The (R)-aminoalkyl heterocycles 39 and 42 were prepared from the ketones 36 and 40, generated in the synthesis of the (R)-hydroxyalkyl heterocycles 16 and 26, respectively. The ketones were converted using Ellmanâs chiral auxiliary to 37 and 41.(37) Sulfinamide 37 was hydrolyzed and N-BOC protected prior to Suzuki coupling with the boronate ester 31, a step that proceeded in a comparable yield to its (R)-hydroxyl alkyl counterpart 16 shown in Scheme 1. Hydrolysis of the ester then afforded the desired acid 39 with the amine N-BOC protected to avoid interference in the subsequent ester formation with tripeptide 44. Quinoline sulfinamide 41 was coupled to the boronate ester 31 in a Suzuki reaction followed by hydrolysis of the sulfinamide to the amine 42. The Suzuki coupling proceeded in 86% yield, which was superior to the Heck procedure used on the corresponding (R)-hydroxyalkyl precursor 26 described in Scheme 2. The methyl ester in 42 was deemed necessary to avoid any competition of this acid in the subsequent amide bond forming reaction with the piperazic acid. The tripeptide 44 was prepared in two steps from the TCE protected piperazic ester 43(38) starting with N-BOC deprotection and then EDC coupling with the hydroxy substituted butanoic acid. Quinoline 34 was prepared from 44 by removal of the TCE ester and coupling to amine 42 to generate 45. Methyl ester hydrolysis and macrolactonization at C13 proceeded in 45% yield to provide 34. Isoquinoline 35 was prepared in a reverse sequence beginning with ester formation on tripeptide 44 to form 46, followed by TCE and N-BOC deprotection, and finally C1 macrolactamization. The unoptimized yield of 35 was only 3% over the final three steps and therefore inferior to the C13 Shiina macrolactonization procedure for compound 34.
    Naphthyl 18 and quinoline 19 were both more lipophilic than isoquinoline 8, leading to improved Caco-2 permeability (Table 2, column 8). The increased lipophilicity of 19 is thought to be driven by the placement of the heterocyclic nitrogen directed toward the âinteriorâ of the macrocycle that minimizes its impact on overall polarity. Consistent with this hypothesis, the two quinoline regioisomers of 19 in which the quinoline nitrogen is situated on the âexteriorâ of the macrocycle showed reduced lipophilicty by at least 0.8 log D units relative to 19 (data not shown). The addition of the C14 gem-dimethyl groups, quinoline 20 and isoquinoline 21, further improved permeability relative to the respective C14 unsubstituted counterparts. Moreover, the methyl groups also drove a 2â3 fold improved binding affinity and HCV replicon potency. Unfortunately, oral bioavailability in dog when dosed as a solution was <10% for 20 and only marginally improved for 21. Investigation of the physicochemical properties of 20 and 21 was instructive and revealed that both compounds were poorly soluble as amorphous solids, with an aqueous solubility limit <6 Î¼g/mL at pH 7.2, and also highly crystalline with melting points of 277 Â°C, and 310 Â°C, respectively. This led to the conclusion that oral bioavailability was likely limited due to high crystallinity and moderate to low permeability, potentially leading to precipitation of compound in the intestine. In an attempt to reduce crystallinity, extensive modifications were explored to destabilize crystal packing forces and also increase polarity, including alternative side chains to alanine or valine, or modifications to the C14 gem-dimethyl groups. These changes were unsuccessful since the addition of polar groups, thereby increasing the number of hydrogen bond donor/acceptors, resulted in a significant loss of permeability. The solution to this dilemma was to first elevate the permeability of the macrocycle to a more significant extent by altering the environment of one of the three NH hydrogen bond donors in the core of the macrocycle, and then address solubility with polar groups. Replacement of the piperazic acid NH or the alanine NH was not feasible due to their critical role in binding, thus leaving the remaining valine NH as the most logical choice for manipulation. Simple methyl substitution of the nitrogen was found to reduce potency (compound not shown). The breakthrough compound was 34, in which the C13 lactam and C1 lactone groups of quinoline 20 are switched with each other (Table 2). The significant improvement in apical to basolateral permeability was not unexpected because of the potential for an internal hydrogen bond to form between the C1 lactam NH and the quinoline nitrogen.(39â41) The presence of the internal hydrogen bond for 34 in lipophilic environments is evidenced by two factors, first the 0.5 unit increase in log D relative to 20, and second the stark contrast in permeability properties relative to isoquinoline 35. Isoquinoline 35 has no potential for an internal hydrogen bond and relative to 34 has a > 1 unit lower log D, very poor apical to basolateral permeability, and high efflux (ratio of BA/AB = 79 fold, Table 2). It is also worth noting that the measured log D for compound 20 was reliably predicted by calculated methods, but 34 was underpredicted by 0.6 units. This highlights the importance of employing measured log D data during the SAR optimization process, especially for constrained molecules where dynamic hydrogen bonds can impact lipophilicity. NMR experiments conducted on 3, an optimized C1 lactam described in the next phase of optimization, provided evidence for the presence of an intramolecular hydrogen bond in lipophilic environments (see Supporting Information). Given that permeability was successfully addressed in 34, we compared its solubility properties with 35, expecting that its higher log D would make it less soluble. However, the aqueous dissolution limit of 34 and 35 from amorphous solids demonstrated a surprisingly favorable solubility limit of 55 Î¼g/mL for 34 compared to 6 Î¼g/mL for 35 in aqueous media. This rare finding was presumed to be due to the flexibility of the macrocycle or âchameleonic effectâ that allowed it to readily adopt alternate conformations in more polar environments. The âchameleonicâ behavior of macrocyclic compounds adapting to different environments through making or breaking of intramolecular hydrogen bonds has been noted as drug delivery tactic for compounds in chemical space beyond the rule of 5.(42,43) Gratifyingly, 34 dosed as an oral solution in rat and dog demonstrated excellent absorption properties leading to high oral bioavailability (Table 3). Moreover, the predicted clearance from in vitro hepatocyte incubations nicely correlated with the in vivo measured clearance. An additional attractive feature of 34 is the esterase labile lactone group is now adjacent to the C14 gem-dimethyl groups and therefore more hindered than it was at the C1 position in 20. Thus, the plasma stability of 34 relative to 20 also increased, especially in rat, which has high plasma esterase activity (Table 2, column 6).(44) This is an important feature because it eliminates or reduces a key metabolic pathway in rodents, allowing these compounds to be deployed in rodent disease models.

    X-ray structures of quinolines 19, 20, and 34 complexed to Cyp A were generated to probe differences in the binding mode relative to 8 (Figure 2CâE). The most noticeable difference in the structure of quinoline 19 relative to isoquinoline 8 (panel C) was the orientation of the trans double bond in the C14âC16 region. The small change in the position of the quinoline ring over the R55 residue likely drives the alternative orientation of the trans double bond. A 3-fold improvement in binding affinity was observed with the introduction of the C14 gem dimethyl groups in analogue 20 compared to 19. The overlay of 19 and 20 (Figure 2D) revealed that the macrocycle 20 sits closer to the protein surface, similar to that observed for 2, and the C14 pro R methyl forms a favorable lipophilic interaction with threonine 73. The structure of compound 20 also illustrates that the C1 lactone oxygen and the C13 lactam NH are not involved in direct interactions with the protein, supporting the hypothesis that these groups are potentially interchangeable without potency differences, as was found in 34 and 35 (Table 2). The overlay of 34 in Cyp A with 20 (Figure 2E) confirms that both compounds have a similar binding mode. An interesting observation in the structure of 34 is that the intramolecular hydrogen bond between the C1 NH and the quinoline nitrogen is not present when binding to Cyp A. This is consistent with the macrocycle adopting a low energy conformation without the intramolecular hydrogen bond in polar environments such as the Cyp active site, and its improved aqueous solubility.
    The breakthrough in oral bioavailability prompted efforts to address the remaining liabilities of 34, which included moderate to high metabolic clearance (Table 4, column 6) and significant PXR activation potential (Table 4, column 8).(45) Activation of PXR raised our concerns for potential drugâdrug interactions, especially important when considering combination regimens with other drugs. The strategy employed in this final phase was to revisit replacements of the C14 gem-dimethyl groups with the goal of introducing polar groups to lower log D and reduce oxidative metabolism and PXR activation. The structure of 34 established that the C14 gem-dimethyl groups remained in the same pose as compound 20 and were therefore suitable branch points for these modifications that would not alter binding potency (Figure 2E). The introduction of polar groups was still expected to erode permeability, but given the high intrinsic permeability of 34 we reasoned that the permeability would remain in a satisfactory range, and likely benefits would be gained in solubility. To keep the overall synthesis succinct, an emphasis was placed on C14 substituents that were readily accessed as illustrated by targets 3 and 47â54 (Table 4).

    Compounds 47â50 were prepared via macrolactamization at C1 (Scheme 5) according to the same method described for 35. The quinoline prescursors 56aâc were generated by Heck coupling of bromide 55 with spiro substituted substituted but-3-enoic acids/esters 58, 59, and 61 in 59â89% yield. The ketone 58 was converted to the methoxy spiro acid 59 by borohydride reduction, followed by methylation of the major trans product, and hydrolysis. The pyran ester 61 was prepared by alkylation of 60 with vinyl bromide. The ketone group in precursor 56a was also reduced to its cis/trans hydroxy mixture, the ester then hydrolyzed to the acid, followed by O-silyl protection of the hydroxyl to provide intermediate 57. Following a similar procedure to that described for 35, the quinoline precursors 56bâc and 57 were coupled with tripeptide 44 to generate intermediates 62aâc, deprotected to generate the seco acids, and then subjected to macrolactamization at C1 to form 47â50. Similar to 35, the yields for 47â49 were <10% over the final sequence from 62aâc but a significantly improved yield of 45% was achieved for 50 by the addition of catalytic DMAP. Despite the improved yield for 50 a two-step route using the key bromide intermediate 63 and macrolactonization at C13 was used to generate compounds 3 and 51â54 (Scheme 6). The spiro acid 65 was generated from thiopyran 63, while ethyl acetoactete 66 served as the starting material to both the unsubstituted and substituted 1,3-dioxane acids 68a and 68b, respectively. The 1,4-dioxepane 69 was transformed via the nitroalkene 70 to the desired acid 71. The formation of the seco acids for C13 macrolactonization began with the coupling of the quinoline bromide 72, generated in the synthesis of 55, onto the tripeptide 44, followed by Heck coupling of the spiro but-3-enoic acid 65, 68a/b or 71. Without purification, the seco-acid products were then subjected to macrolactonization using the Shiina conditions at concentrations of â¼3 mmol to provide 3 and 51â53. Overall the yields for the two-step sequence were modest ranging from 7 to 30%, the highest for compound 3. The synthesis of 3 was therefore achieved in a convergent four-step synthesis from tripeptide 44 with an unoptimized yield of 17%. Compound 52 also served as the precursor to 54 that was realized upon treatment of 52 with acid.
    The potency, metabolic, and PXR activation properties of the C14 modified analogues are compared to 34 in Table 4. In general, binding affinity was comparable or up to 2-fold more potent than 34 for most analogues, and HCV replicon was generally EC50 < 100 nM. As anticipated from prior modeling, the X-ray complex of 3 with Cyp A confirmed that the overall binding mode was approximately the same as the gem-dimethyl analogue 34, with the 1,3-dioxane ring positioned in the same geometry as the methyl groups (Figure 2F). All the compounds demonstrated low protein binding shifts relative to 34 (Table 4, column 5), with compound 3 demonstrating a calculated paEC50 = 240 nM. The predicted microsomal clearance, permeability, and PXR activation potential trended in line with the lipophilicity of the compounds. Compounds 49 and 50 with the higher log D values had the best permeability properties, but also high metabolic clearance and PXR activation potential comparable to 34. In contrast, the compounds with log D â 2.0, e.g., 3, 47â48 and 52, 53, sacrificed some apical to basolateral permeability but tended to be more metabolically stable and have lower PXR activation. Diol 54 bearing two hydrogen bond donors relative to 34 had the lowest log D, and not surprisingly, the poorest permeability. Compound 3 demonstrated the best overall profile and was subsequently selected for in vivo studies.
    To support in vivo studies, several grams of 3 were synthesized using the procedure described in Scheme 6. As indicated earlier, a key component during the SAR optimization toward 3 was to minimize structural complexity and focus on synthetically accessible building blocks that could be readily converged together. The end result is that 3 can be prepared in a short four-step sequence that brings together three key building blocks, 44, 72, and 68a (Figure 3). The three key building blocks in turn can be generated in relatively short sequences from commercially available materials. The efficiency of the route also enables future SAR explorations on the sanglifehrin macrocycle beyond the scope presented here. For example, elaborating the methyl side chain of 72 would allow the inclusion of some of the structural features found in AAE931 (Figure 1). Taken together, 18 synthetic steps are required for the preparation of 3, and the flexibility afforded by total synthesis over semisynthetic methods enables more rapid exploration of sanglifehrin based Cyp A inhibitors.
    Prior to extensive in vivo studies, a hERG patch clamp assessment at physiological temperature was conducted on 3 and showed an IC50 > 100 Î¼M, indicating minimal risk for QT prolongation in vivo. Intravenous dosing of 3 in rat and dog demonstrated low in vivo clearance rates, 2â3-fold better than predicted from the in vitro hepatocyte assays (Table 3). This favorable trend, combined with the good in vitro hepatocyte and in vivo clearance correlation established for 34, suggests that the human hepatocyte clearance prediction of 0.3 L/h/kg for compound 3 is likely a conservative prediction of human in vivo clearance. Additional in vitro and in vivo data in more species would be necessary to derive a more robust human clearance prediction for 3. Following a 5 mg/kg oral dose in dog, the blood concentrations of 3 at 24 h exceeded 1 Î¼M, >4-fold above the paEC50 of 240 nM. Oral bioavailability was 100% in rat and 55% in dog, demonstrating good intestinal permeability properties. The superior plasma-adjusted anti-HCV potency and optimal PK properties of 3 resulted in its selection as an oral candidate for in vivo efficacy studies in a variety of disease models. Moreover, the low projected clearance in humans together with the oral bioavailability data across multiple species supports the potential of 3 to also be used in the clinical setting as an oral inhibitor of Cyps.

    Conclusion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    Cyp inhibition by oral small molecules holds significant promise for the interrogation of Cyps and their role in disease. The current report extends our previous findings on sanglifehrin based macrocyclic inhibitors resulting in the identification of the potent and orally bioavailable Cyp inhibitor 3. Compound 3 has Cyp A Kd = 5 nM, a high plasma free fraction leading to a calculated paEC50 = 240 nM, excellent oral bioavailability above 50% in two preclinical species, and minimal PXR activation potential. Critical advances during the structure driven optimization process were reducing the macrocycle size and combining a quinoline heterocycle with a C1 lactam and C13 lactone switch that dramatically improved permeability through formation of an intramolecular hydrogen bond. Late stage elaboration of the C14 substituent to a spirocyclic1,3-dioxane lowered log D, reduced PXR activation, and improved metabolic stability resulting in the identification of 3 as the best oral inhibitor. Importantly, 3 can be derived from three readily available precursors following a short four-step sequence. Synthesis developments have since been introduced beyond this report to generate 3 on a 100â1000 g scale to enable its use in a variety of preclinical disease models. These advances and results from the models will be reported in due course.

    All research compounds were synthesized at Gilead Sciences Inc. (Foster City, CA, USA) or Selcia Ltd. (Ongar, UK). Commercially available solvents and reagents were used as received without further purification. Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra were recorded on a Bruker 300, 400, or 500 MHz NMR or a Varian Mercury Plus 400 MHz NMR. Chemical shifts are reported in ppm (Î´) using the residual solvent line as internal standard (CDCl3: Î´ 7.26, CD3OD: Î´ 3.31, CD3CN: Î´ 1.94 DMSO-d6: Î´ 2.50). Splitting patterns are designated as s, singlet; d, doublet; t, triplet; q, quartet; p, pentet; m, multiplet; br, broad; ABq, AB quartet; app, apparent. Compound 313C NMR assignment can be found in Supporting Information. All isolated characterized final compounds for assay were determined to have a purity of >95% at 254 nM by HPLC (see Supporting Information). High resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS) and low resolution (unit mass) liquid chromatography/mass spectrometry (LC/MS) details are provided in the Supporting Information. Retention times are denoted as Tr. Preparative normal phase silica gel chromatography was carried out manually or using a Teledyne ISCO CombiFlash Companion instrument with silica gel cartridges.. All in vivo studies were performed in accordance with local IACUC guidelines.

      2
      Cyclophilin and peptidyl-prolyl cis-trans isomerase are probably identical proteins
      Fischer, Gunter; Wittmann-Liebold, Brigitte; Lang, Kurt; Kiefhaber, Thomas; Schmid, Franz X.
      Nature (London, United Kingdom) (1989), 337 (6206), 476-8CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836.
      The N-terminal sequence of peptidyl-prolyl cis-trans isomerase (PPIase), together with further chem. and enzymic properties are reported. The results indicate that this enzyme is probably identical to cyclophilin, a recently discovered mammalian protein which binds tightly to cyclosporin A (CsA). Cyclophilin is thought to be linked to the immunosuppressive action of CsA. The 1st 38 amino acid residues of porcine PPIase and of bovine cyclophilin are identical and the 2 proteins both have a relative mol. mass of â¼17,000. The catalysis of prolyl isomerization in oligopeptides and of protein folding by PPIase are strongly inhibited in the presence of low levels of CsA. The activities of both PPIase and cyclophilin depend on a single SH group. At present it is unknown whether the inhibition of prolyl isomerase activity is related to the immunosuppressive action of CsA.

      Cyclophilin: a specific cytosolic binding protein for cyclosporin A
      Handschumacher, Robert E.; Harding, Matthew W.; Rice, Jeffrey; Drugge, Rhett J.; Speicher, David W.
      Science (Washington, DC, United States) (1984), 226 (4674), 544-7CODEN: SCIEAS; ISSN:0036-8075.
      Cyclophilin (I), a specific cytosolic binding protein responsible for the concn. of the immunosuppressant, cyclosporin A (II), by lymphoid cells, was purified to homogeneity from bovine thymocytes. Cation-exchange HPLC resolved a major and minor I species that bound II with a dissocn. const. of â¼2 Ã 10-7 M and specific activities of 77 and 67 Î¼g/mg protein, resp. Both I species had an apparent mol. wt. of 15,000, a pI of 9.6, and nearly identical amino acid compns. A portion of the N-terminal amino acid sequence of the major species was detd. The II-binding activity of I was SH group-dependent, unstable at 56Â° and at pH 4 or 9.5, and sensitive to trypsin but not to chymotrypsin degrdn. I specifically bound a series of II analogs in proportion to their activity in a mixed lymphocyte reaction. The isolation of I from the cytosol of thymocytes suggested that the immunosuppressive activity of II is mediated by an intracellular mechanism, rather than by a membrane-assocd. mechanism.

      Calcineurin is a common target of cyclophilin-cyclosporin A and FKBP-FK506 complexes
      Liu, Jun; Farmer, Jesse D., Jr.; Lane, Willam S.; Friedman, Jeff; Weissman, Irving; Schreiber, Stuart L.
      Cell (Cambridge, MA, United States) (1991), 66 (4), 807-15CODEN: CELLB5; ISSN:0092-8674.
      Although the immediate receptors (immunophilins) of the immunosuppressants cyclosporin A (CsA) and FK506 are distinct, their similar mechanisms of inhibition of cell signaling suggest that their assocd. immunophilin complexes interact with a common target. The authors report that the complexes cyclophilin-CsA and FKBP-FK506 (but not cyclophilin, FKBP, FKBP-rapamycin, or FKBP-506BD) competitively bind to and inhibit the Ca2+- and calmodulin-dependent phosphatase calcineurin, although the binding and inhibition of calcineurin do not require calmodulin. These results suggest that calcineurin is involved in a common step assocd. with T cell receptor and IgE receptor signaling pathways and that cyclophilin and FKBP mediate the actions of CsA and FK506, resp., by forming drug-dependent complexes with and altering the activity of calcineurin-calmodulin.

      Cyclophilin A: a key player for human disease
      Nigro, P.; Pompilio, G.; Capogrossi, M. C.
      Cell Death & Disease (2013), 4 (Oct.), e888CODEN: CDDEA4; ISSN:2041-4889. (Nature Publishing Group)
      A review. Cyclophilin A (CyPA) is a ubiquitously distributed protein belonging to the immunophilin family. CyPA has peptidyl prolyl cis-trans isomerase (PPIase) activity, which regulates protein folding and trafficking. Although CyPA was initially believed to function primarily as an intracellular protein, recent studies have revealed that it can be secreted by cells in response to inflammatory stimuli. Current research in animal models and humans has provided compelling evidences supporting the crit. function of CyPA in several human diseases. This review discusses recently available data about CyPA in cardiovascular diseases, viral infections, neurodegeneration, cancer, rheumatoid arthritis, sepsis, asthma, periodontitis and aging. It is believed that further elucidations of the role of CyPA will provide a better understanding of the mol. mechanisms underlying these diseases and will help develop novel pharmacol. therapies.

      8
      Cyclophilin inhibition as potential therapy for liver diseases
      Naoumov, Nikolai V.
      Journal of Hepatology (2014), 61 (5), 1166-1174CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. The cyclophilins are a group of proteins with peptidyl-prolyl isomerase enzymic activity, localized in different cellular compartments and involved in a variety of functions related to cell metab. and energy homeostasis, having enhanced expression in inflammation or malignancy. Cyclophilin A (CypA), the most abundantly expressed cyclophilin, is present mainly in the cytoplasm and is a host factor involved in the life cycle of multiple viruses. The extracellular fractions of CypA and CypB are potent pro-inflammatory mediators. CypD, located in mitochondria, is a key regulator of mitochondrial permeability transition pores, and is crit. for necrotic cell death. Cyclosporines are the prototype cyclophilin inhibitors. Cyclic peptides, which bind and inhibit cyclophilins without having immunosuppressive properties, have been generated by chem. modifications of cyclosporin A. In addn., cyclophilin inhibitors that are structurally different from cyclosporines have been synthesized. The involvement of cyclophilins in the pathogenesis of different liver diseases has been established using both in vitro and in vivo investigations, thus indicating that cyclophilin inhibition may be of therapeutic benefit. This review summarizes the evidence for potential therapeutic applications of non-immunosuppressive cyclophilin inhibitors, alone or in combination with other agents, in virus-induced liver diseases like hepatitis C, B or Delta, liver inflammation and fibrosis, acetaminophen-induced liver toxicity and hepatocellular carcinoma.

A greater understanding of the physiol. functions of the MPTP and its regulation by CypD will likely suggest novel therapeutic approaches for cardiovascular disease; both dependent and independent of programmed necrotic cell death mechanisms.

      10
      The role of immunophilins in viral infection
      Hopkins, Sam; Gallay, Philippe A.
      Biochimica et Biophysica Acta, General Subjects (2015), 1850 (10), 2103-2110CODEN: BBGSB3; ISSN:0304-4165. (Elsevier B.V.)
      A review. Tremendous progress has been made in the past 20 years in understanding the roles played by immunophilins, and in particular the cyclophilins, in supporting the replication cycles of human viruses. A growing body of genetic and biochem. evidence and data from clin. trials confirm that cyclophilins are essential cofactors that contribute to establishing a permissive environment within the host cell that supports the replication of HIV-1 and HCV. Cyclophilin A regulates HIV-1 replication kinetics and infectivity, modulates sensitivity to host restriction factors, and cooperates in the transit of the pre-integration complex into the nucleus of infected cells. Cyclophilin A is an essential cofactor whose expression supports HCV-specific RNA replication in human hepatocytes. Peptidyl-prolyl isomerase inhibitors have been used in clin. trials to validate cyclophilins as antiviral targets for the treatment of HIV-1 and Chronic Hepatitis C virus infection and as mol. probes to identify the roles played by immunophilins in supporting the replication cycles of human viruses. This review summarizes emerging research that defines the functions of immunophilins in supporting the replication cycles of HIV-1, HCV, HBV, coronaviruses, and other viral pathogens and describes new information that suggests a role for immunophilins in regulating innate immune responses against chronic viral infection. The dependence on cyclophilins by evolutionarily distinct viruses for accomplishing various steps in replication such as viral entry, initiation of genomic nucleic acid replication, viral genome uncoating, nuclear import and nuclear entry, emphasizes the potential of cyclophilin inhibitors as therapeutic agents. This article is part of a Special Issue entitled Proline-directed Foldases: Cell Signaling Catalysts and Drug Targets.

      The cyclophilin inhibitor Debio-O25 shows potent anti-hepatitis C effect in patients coinfected with hepatitis C and human immunodeficiency virus
      Flisiak, Robert; Horban, Andrzej; Gallay, Philippe; Bobardt, Michael; Selvarajah, Suganya; Wiercinska-Drapalo, Alicja; Siwak, Ewa; Cielniak, Iwona; Higersberger, Jozef; Kierkus, Jarek; Aeschlimann, Christian; Grosgurin, Pierre; Nicolas-Metral, Valerie; Dumont, Jean-Maurice; Porchet, Herve; Crabbe, Raf; Scalfaro, Pietro
      Hepatology (Hoboken, NJ, United States) (2008), 47 (3), 817-826CODEN: HPTLD9; ISSN:0270-9139. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      Debio-025 is an oral cyclophilin (Cyp) inhibitor with potent anti-hepatitis C virus activity in vitro. Its effect on viral load as well as its influence on intracellular Cyp levels was investigated in a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled study. Mean hepatitis C viral load decreased significantly by 3.6 log10 after a 14-day oral treatment with 1200 mg twice daily (P < 0.0001) with an effect against the 3 genotypes (1, 3, and 4) represented in the study. In addn., the absence of viral rebound during treatment indicates that Debio-025 has a high barrier for the selection of resistance. In Debio-025-treated patients, cydophilin B (CypB) levels in peripheral blood mononuclear cells decreased from 67 Â± 6 (std. error) ng/mg protein (baseline) to 5 Â± 1 ng/mg protein at day 15 (P < 0.01). Conclusion: Debio-025 induced a strong drop in CypB levels, coinciding with the decrease in hepatitis C viral load. These are the first preliminary human data supporting the hypothesis that CypB may play an important role in hepatitis C virus replication and that Cyp inhibition is a valid target for the development of anti-hepatitis C drugs.

      The cyclophilin inhibitor SCY-635 suppresses viral replication and induces endogenous interferons in patients with chronic HCV genotype 1 infection
      Hopkins, Sam; DiMassimo, Betty; Rusnak, Pamela; Heuman, Douglas; Lalezari, Jacob; Sluder, Ann; Scorneaux, Bernard; Mosier, Sarah; Kowalczyk, Paul; Ribeill, Yves; Baugh, James; Gallay, Philippe
      Journal of Hepatology (2012), 57 (1), 47-54CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Background & Aims: SCY-635 is a non-immunosuppressive analog of cyclosporin A that inhibits cyclophilins A and B and hepatitis C virus (HCV) replication in vitro. In a phase 1b multi-dose escalation study, we evaluated the safety, plasma pharmacokinetics, and antiviral activity of 15 days of monotherapy with SCY-635 in adults with chronic genotype 1 HCV infection. Methods: Twenty adults with chronic HCV genotype 1 were randomized to SCY-635 oral doses of 100, 200, or 300 mg three times daily for 15 days. Results: No dose-limiting clin. or lab. toxicities were identified. On day 15, the mean decline in plasma viremia was 2.24 Â± 1.74 log10 IU/mL with SCY-635 900 mg/d. Individual antiviral responses correlated with host IL28B genotype. Post hoc analyses indicated treatment with SCY-635 increased plasma protein concns. of interferon Î± (IFNÎ±), IFNs Î»1 and Î»3, and 2'5' oligoadenylate synthetase 1 (2'5'OAS-1), with the greatest increases in IL28B CC and CT subjects. Changes in plasma concns. for all markers were coincident with changes in the plasma concn. of SCY-635. Peaks of IFNs Î±, Î»1, and Î»3 and 2'5'OAS-1 were obsd. within 2 h after drug administration. In replicon cells, SCY-635 enhanced secretion of type I and type III IFNs and increased the expression of IFN-stimulated genes (ISG). Conclusions: These studies establish clin. proof of concept for SCY-635 as a novel antiviral agent and suggest that restoration of the host innate immune response to chronic hepatitis C infection may represent a major mechanism through which cyclophilin inhibitors exert clin. antiviral activity.

      Cyclosporin A inhibits hepatitis B and hepatitis D virus entry by cyclophilin-independent interference with the NTCP receptor
      Nkongolo, Shirin; Ni, Yi; Lempp, Florian A.; Kaufman, Christina; Lindner, Thomas; Esser-Nobis, Katharina; Lohmann, Volker; Mier, Walter; Mehrle, Stefan; Urban, Stephan
      Journal of Hepatology (2014), 60 (4), 723-731CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Chronic hepatitis B and hepatitis D are global health problems caused by the human hepatitis B and hepatitis D virus. The myristoylated preS1 domain of the large envelope protein mediates specific binding to hepatocytes by sodium taurocholate co-transporting polypeptide (NTCP). NTCP is a bile salt transporter known to be inhibited by cyclosporin A. This study aimed to characterize the effect of cyclosporin A on HBV/HDV infection. HepaRG cells, primary human hepatocytes, and susceptible NTCP-expressing hepatoma cell lines were applied for infection expts. The mode of action of cyclosporin A was studied by comparing the effect of different inhibitors, cyclophilin A/B/C-silenced cell lines as well as NTCP variants and mutants. Bile salt transporter and HBV receptor functions were investigated by taurocholate uptake and quantification of HBVpreS binding.Cyclosporin A inhibited hepatitis B and D virus infections during and - less pronounced - prior to virus inoculation. Binding of HBVpreS to NTCP was blocked by cyclosporin A concns. at 8 Î¼M. An NTCP variant deficient in HBVpreS binding but competent for bile salt transport showed resistance to cyclosporin A. Silencing of cyclophilins A/B/C did not abrogate transporter and receptor inhibition. In contrast, tacrolimus, a cyclophilin-independent calcineurin inhibitor, was inactive.HBV and HDV entry via sodium taurocholate co-transporting polypeptide is inhibited by cyclosporin A. The interaction between the drug and the viral receptor is direct and overlaps with a functional binding site of the preS1 domain, which mediates viral entry.

      21
      From Chemical Tools to Clinical Medicines: Nonimmunosuppressive Cyclophilin Inhibitors Derived from the Cyclosporin and Sanglifehrin Scaffolds
      Sweeney, Zachary K.; Fu, Jiping; Wiedmann, Brigitte
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 57 (17), 7145-7159CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      A review. The cyclophilins are widely expressed enzymes that catalyze the interconversion of the cis and trans peptide bonds of prolines. The immunosuppressive natural products cyclosporine A and sanglifehrin A inhibit the enzymic activity of the cyclophilins. Chem. modification of both the cyclosporine and sanglifehrin scaffolds has produced many analogs that inhibit cyclophilins in vitro but have reduced immunosuppressive properties. Three nonimmunosuppressive cyclophilin inhibitors (alisporivir, SCY-635, and NIM811) have demonstrated clin. efficacy for the treatment of hepatitis C infection. Addnl. candidates are in various stages of preclin. development for the treatment of hepatitis C or myocardial reperfusion injury. Recent publications suggest that cyclophilin inhibitors may have utility for the treatment of diverse viral infections, inflammatory indications, and cancer. In this review, we document the structure-activity relationships of the nonimmunosuppressive cyclosporins and sanglifehrins in clin. and preclin. development. Aspects of the pharmacokinetic behavior and chem. biol. of these drug candidates are also described.

      Potent Nonimmunosuppressive Cyclophilin Inhibitors With Improved Pharmaceutical Properties and Decreased Transporter Inhibition
      Fu, Jiping; Tjandra, Meiliana; Becker, Christopher; Bednarczyk, Dallas; Capparelli, Michael; Elling, Robert; Hanna, Imad; Fujimoto, Roger; Furegati, Markus; Karur, Subramanian; Kasprzyk, Theresa; Knapp, Mark; Leung, Kwan; Li, Xiaolin; Lu, Peichao; Mergo, Wosenu; Miault, Charlotte; Ng, Simon; Parker, David; Peng, Yunshan; Roggo, Silvio; Rivkin, Alexey; Simmons, Robert L.; Wang, Michael; Wiedmann, Brigitte; Weiss, Andrew H.; Xiao, Linda; Xie, Lili; Xu, Wenjian; Yifru, Aregahegn; Yang, Shengtian; Zhou, Bo; Sweeney, Zachary K.
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 57 (20), 8503-8516CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Nonimmunosuppressive cyclophilin inhibitors have demonstrated efficacy for the treatment of hepatitis C infection (HCV). However, alisporivir, cyclosporin A, and most other cyclosporins are potent inhibitors of OATP1B1, MRP2, MDR1, and other important drug transporters. Redn. of the side chain hydrophobicity of the P4 residue preserves cyclophilin binding and antiviral potency while decreasing transporter inhibition. Representative inhibitor 33 (NIM258) is a less potent transporter inhibitor relative to previously described cyclosporins, retains anti-HCV activity in cell culture, and has an acceptable pharmacokinetic profile in rats and dogs. An X-ray structure of 33 bound to rat cyclophilin D is reported.

      Bioengineering and Semisynthesis of an Optimized Cyclophilin Inhibitor for Treatment of Chronic Viral Infection
      Hansson, Magnus Joakim; Moss, Steven James; Bobardt, Michael; Chatterji, Udayan; Coates, Nigel; Garcia-Rivera, Jose A.; Elmer, Eskil; Kendrew, Steve; Leyssen, Pieter; Neyts, Johan; Nur-E-Alam, Mohammad; Warneck, Tony; Wilkinson, Barrie; Gallay, Philippe; Gregory, Matthew Alan
      Chemistry & Biology (Oxford, United Kingdom) (2015), 22 (2), 285-292CODEN: CBOLE2; ISSN:1074-5521. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      Inhibition of host-encoded targets, such as the cyclophilins, provides an opportunity to generate potent high barrier to resistance antivirals for the treatment of a broad range of viral diseases. However, many host-targeted agents are natural products, which can be difficult to optimize using synthetic chem. alone. We describe the orthogonal combination of bioengineering and semisynthetic chem. to optimize the drug-like properties of sanglifehrin A, a known cyclophilin inhibitor of mixed nonribosomal peptide/polyketide origin, to generate the drug candidate NVP018 (formerly BC556). NVP018 is a potent inhibitor of hepatitis B virus, hepatitis C virus (HCV), and HIV-1 replication, shows minimal inhibition of major drug transporters, and has a high barrier to generation of both HCV and HIV-1 resistance.

      Fragment-based discovery of a new family of non-peptidic small-molecule cyclophilin inhibitors with potent antiviral activities
      Ahmed-Belkacem, Abdelhakim; Colliandre, Lionel; Ahnou, Nazim; Nevers, Quentin; Gelin, Muriel; Bessin, Yannick; Brillet, Rozenn; Cala, Olivier; Douguet, Dominique; Bourguet, William; Krimm, Isabelle; Pawlotsky, Jean-Michel; Guichou, Jean- Francois
      Nature Communications (2016), 7 (), 12777CODEN: NCAOBW; ISSN:2041-1723. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Cyclophilins are peptidyl-prolyl cis/trans isomerases (PPIase) that catalyze the interconversion of the peptide bond at proline residues. Several cyclophilins play a pivotal role in the life cycle of a no. of viruses. The existing cyclophilin inhibitors, all derived from cyclosporine A or sanglifehrin A, have disadvantages, including their size, potential for side effects unrelated to cyclophilin inhibition and drug-drug interactions, unclear antiviral spectrum and manufg. issues. Here we use a fragment-based drug discovery approach using nucleic magnetic resonance, X-ray crystallog. and structure-based compd. optimization to generate a new family of non-peptidic, small-mol. cyclophilin inhibitors with potent in vitro PPIase inhibitory activity and antiviral activity against hepatitis C virus, human immunodeficiency virus and coronaviruses. This family of compds. has the potential for broad-spectrum, high-barrier-to-resistance treatment of viral infections.

      40
      Cell permeability beyond the rule of 5
      Matsson, Paer; Doak, Bradley C.; Over, Bjoern; Kihlberg, Jan
      Advanced Drug Delivery Reviews (2016), 101 (), 42-61CODEN: ADDREP; ISSN:0169-409X. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Drug discovery for difficult targets that have large and flat binding sites is often better suited to compds. beyond the "rule of 5" (bRo5). However, such compds. carry higher pharmacokinetic risks, such as low soly. and permeability, and increased efflux and metab. Interestingly, recent drug approvals and studies suggest that cell permeable and orally bioavailable drugs can be discovered far into bRo5 space. Tactics such as redn. or shielding of polarity by N-methylation, bulky side chains and intramol. hydrogen bonds may be used to increase cell permeability in this space, but often results in decreased soly. Conformationally flexible compds. can, however, combine high permeability and soly., properties that are keys for cell permeability and intestinal absorption. Recent developments in computational conformational anal. will aid design of such compds. and hence prediction of cell permeability. Transporter mediated efflux occurs for most investigated drugs in bRo5 space, however it is commonly overcome by high local intestinal concns. on oral administration. In contrast, there is little data to support significant impact of transporter-mediated intestinal absorption in bRo5 space. Current knowledge of compd. properties that govern transporter effects of bRo5 drugs is limited and requires further fundamental and comprehensive studies.

      Conformational Flexibility, Internal Hydrogen Bonding, and Passive Membrane Permeability: Successful in Silico Prediction of the Relative Permeabilities of Cyclic Peptides
      Rezai, Taha; Bock, Jonathan E.; Zhou, Mai V.; Kalyanaraman, Chakrapani; Lokey, R. Scott; Jacobson, Matthew P.
      Journal of the American Chemical Society (2006), 128 (43), 14073-14080CODEN: JACSAT; ISSN:0002-7863. (American Chemical Society)
      We report an atomistic phys. model for the passive membrane permeability of cyclic peptides. The computational modeling was performed in advance of the expts. and did not involve the use of "training data". The model explicitly treats the conformational flexibility of the peptides by extensive conformational sampling in low (membrane) and high (water) dielec. environments. The passive membrane permeabilities of 11 cyclic peptides were obtained exptl. using a parallel artificial membrane permeability assay (PAMPA) and showed a linear correlation with the computational results with R2 = 0.96. In general, the results support the hypothesis, already well established in the literature, that the ability to form internal hydrogen bonds is crit. for passive membrane permeability and can be the distinguishing factor among closely related compds., such as those studied here. However, we have found that the no. of internal hydrogen bonds that can form in the membrane and the solvent-exposed polar surface area correlate more poorly with PAMPA permeability than our model, which quant. ests. the solvation free energy losses upon moving from high-dielec. water to the low-dielec. interior of a membrane.

        2
        Cyclophilin and peptidyl-prolyl cis-trans isomerase are probably identical proteins
        Fischer, Gunter; Wittmann-Liebold, Brigitte; Lang, Kurt; Kiefhaber, Thomas; Schmid, Franz X.
        Nature (London, United Kingdom) (1989), 337 (6206), 476-8CODEN: NATUAS; ISSN:0028-0836.
        The N-terminal sequence of peptidyl-prolyl cis-trans isomerase (PPIase), together with further chem. and enzymic properties are reported. The results indicate that this enzyme is probably identical to cyclophilin, a recently discovered mammalian protein which binds tightly to cyclosporin A (CsA). Cyclophilin is thought to be linked to the immunosuppressive action of CsA. The 1st 38 amino acid residues of porcine PPIase and of bovine cyclophilin are identical and the 2 proteins both have a relative mol. mass of â¼17,000. The catalysis of prolyl isomerization in oligopeptides and of protein folding by PPIase are strongly inhibited in the presence of low levels of CsA. The activities of both PPIase and cyclophilin depend on a single SH group. At present it is unknown whether the inhibition of prolyl isomerase activity is related to the immunosuppressive action of CsA.

        Cyclophilin: a specific cytosolic binding protein for cyclosporin A
        Handschumacher, Robert E.; Harding, Matthew W.; Rice, Jeffrey; Drugge, Rhett J.; Speicher, David W.
        Science (Washington, DC, United States) (1984), 226 (4674), 544-7CODEN: SCIEAS; ISSN:0036-8075.
        Cyclophilin (I), a specific cytosolic binding protein responsible for the concn. of the immunosuppressant, cyclosporin A (II), by lymphoid cells, was purified to homogeneity from bovine thymocytes. Cation-exchange HPLC resolved a major and minor I species that bound II with a dissocn. const. of â¼2 Ã 10-7 M and specific activities of 77 and 67 Î¼g/mg protein, resp.         Calcineurin is a common target of cyclophilin-cyclosporin A and FKBP-FK506 complexes
        Liu, Jun; Farmer, Jesse D., Jr.; Lane, Willam S.; Friedman, Jeff; Weissman, Irving; Schreiber, Stuart L.
        Cell (Cambridge, MA, United States) (1991), 66 (4), 807-15CODEN: CELLB5; ISSN:0092-8674.
        Although the immediate receptors (immunophilins) of the immunosuppressants cyclosporin A (CsA) and FK506 are distinct, their similar mechanisms of inhibition of cell signaling suggest that their assocd. immunophilin complexes interact with a common target. The authors report that the complexes cyclophilin-CsA and FKBP-FK506 (but not cyclophilin, FKBP, FKBP-rapamycin, or FKBP-506BD) competitively bind to and inhibit the Ca2+- and calmodulin-dependent phosphatase calcineurin, although the binding and inhibition of calcineurin do not require calmodulin. These results suggest that calcineurin is involved in a common step assocd. with T cell receptor and IgE receptor signaling pathways and that cyclophilin and FKBP mediate the actions of CsA and FK506, resp., by forming drug-dependent complexes with and altering the activity of calcineurin-calmodulin.

        Cyclophilin A: a key player for human disease
        Nigro, P.; Pompilio, G.; Capogrossi, M. C.
        Cell Death & Disease (2013), 4 (Oct.), e888CODEN: CDDEA4; ISSN:2041-4889. (Nature Publishing Group)
        A review. Cyclophilin A (CyPA) is a ubiquitously distributed protein belonging to the immunophilin family. CyPA has peptidyl prolyl cis-trans isomerase (PPIase) activity, which regulates protein folding and trafficking. Although CyPA was initially believed to function primarily as an intracellular protein, recent studies have revealed that it can be secreted by cells in response to inflammatory stimuli. Current research in animal models and humans has provided compelling evidences supporting the crit. function of CyPA in several human diseases. This review discusses recently available data about CyPA in cardiovascular diseases, viral infections, neurodegeneration, cancer, rheumatoid arthritis, sepsis, asthma, periodontitis and aging. It is believed that further elucidations of the role of CyPA will provide a better understanding of the mol. mechanisms underlying these diseases and will help develop novel pharmacol. therapies.

        Naoumov, N. V. Cyclophilin inhibition as potential therapy for liver diseases. J. Hepatol. 2014, 61, 1166â 1174, Â DOI: 10.1016/j.jhep.2014.07.008

        8
        Cyclophilin inhibition as potential therapy for liver diseases
        Naoumov, Nikolai V.
        Journal of Hepatology (2014), 61 (5), 1166-1174CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
        A review. The cyclophilins are a group of proteins with peptidyl-prolyl isomerase enzymic activity, localized in different cellular compartments and involved in a variety of functions related to cell metab. and energy homeostasis, having enhanced expression in inflammation or malignancy. Cyclophilin A (CypA), the most abundantly expressed cyclophilin, is present mainly in the cytoplasm and is a host factor involved in the life cycle of multiple viruses. The extracellular fractions of CypA and CypB are potent pro-inflammatory mediators. CypD, located in mitochondria, is a key regulator of mitochondrial permeability transition pores, and is crit. for necrotic cell death. Cyclosporines are the prototype cyclophilin inhibitors. Cyclic peptides, which bind and inhibit cyclophilins without having immunosuppressive properties, have been generated by chem. modifications of cyclosporin A. In addn., cyclophilin inhibitors that are structurally different from cyclosporines have been synthesized. The involvement of cyclophilins in the pathogenesis of different liver diseases has been established using both in vitro and in vivo investigations, thus indicating that cyclophilin inhibition may be of therapeutic benefit. This review summarizes the evidence for potential therapeutic applications of non-immunosuppressive cyclophilin inhibitors, alone or in combination with other agents, in virus-induced liver diseases like hepatitis C, B or Delta, liver inflammation and fibrosis, acetaminophen-induced liver toxicity and hepatocellular carcinoma.

        10
        The role of immunophilins in viral infection
        Hopkins, Sam; Gallay, Philippe A.
        Biochimica et Biophysica Acta, General Subjects (2015), 1850 (10), 2103-2110CODEN: BBGSB3; ISSN:0304-4165. (Elsevier B.V.)
        A review. Tremendous progress has been made in the past 20 years in understanding the roles played by immunophilins, and in particular the cyclophilins, in supporting the replication cycles of human viruses. A growing body of genetic and biochem. evidence and data from clin. trials confirm that cyclophilins are essential cofactors that contribute to establishing a permissive environment within the host cell that supports the replication of HIV-1 and HCV. Cyclophilin A regulates HIV-1 replication kinetics and infectivity, modulates sensitivity to host restriction factors, and cooperates in the transit of the pre-integration complex into the nucleus of infected cells. Cyclophilin A is an essential cofactor whose expression supports HCV-specific RNA replication in human hepatocytes. Peptidyl-prolyl isomerase inhibitors have been used in clin. trials to validate cyclophilins as antiviral targets for the treatment of HIV-1 and Chronic Hepatitis C virus infection and as mol. probes to identify the roles played by immunophilins in supporting the replication cycles of human viruses. This review summarizes emerging research that defines the functions of immunophilins in supporting the replication cycles of HIV-1, HCV, HBV, coronaviruses, and other viral pathogens and describes new information that suggests a role for immunophilins in regulating innate immune responses against chronic viral infection. The dependence on cyclophilins by evolutionarily distinct viruses for accomplishing various steps in replication such as viral entry, initiation of genomic nucleic acid replication, viral genome uncoating, nuclear import and nuclear entry, emphasizes the potential of cyclophilin inhibitors as therapeutic agents. This article is part of a Special Issue entitled Proline-directed Foldases: Cell Signaling Catalysts and Drug Targets.

        The cyclophilin inhibitor Debio-O25 shows potent anti-hepatitis C effect in patients coinfected with hepatitis C and human immunodeficiency virus
        Flisiak, Robert; Horban, Andrzej; Gallay, Philippe; Bobardt, Michael; Selvarajah, Suganya; Wiercinska-Drapalo, Alicja; Siwak, Ewa; Cielniak, Iwona; Higersberger, Jozef; Kierkus, Jarek; Aeschlimann, Christian; Grosgurin, Pierre; Nicolas-Metral, Valerie; Dumont, Jean-Maurice; Porchet, Herve; Crabbe, Raf; Scalfaro, Pietro
        Hepatology (Hoboken, NJ, United States) (2008), 47 (3), 817-826CODEN: HPTLD9; ISSN:0270-9139. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
        Debio-025 is an oral cyclophilin (Cyp) inhibitor with potent anti-hepatitis C virus activity in vitro. Its effect on viral load as well as its influence on intracellular Cyp levels was investigated in a randomized, double-blind, placebo-controlled study. Mean hepatitis C viral load decreased significantly by 3.6 log10 after a 14-day oral treatment with 1200 mg twice daily (P < 0.0001) with an effect against the 3 genotypes (1, 3, and 4) represented in the study. In addn., the absence of viral rebound during treatment indicates that Debio-025 has a high barrier for the selection of resistance. In Debio-025-treated patients, cydophilin B (CypB) levels in peripheral blood mononuclear cells decreased from 67 Â± 6 (std. error) ng/mg protein (baseline) to 5 Â± 1 ng/mg protein at day 15 (P < 0.01). Conclusion: Debio-025 induced a strong drop in CypB levels, coinciding with the decrease in hepatitis C viral load. These are the first preliminary human data supporting the hypothesis that CypB may play an important role in hepatitis C virus replication and that Cyp inhibition is a valid target for the development of anti-hepatitis C drugs.

        The cyclophilin inhibitor SCY-635 suppresses viral replication and induces endogenous interferons in patients with chronic HCV genotype 1 infection
        Hopkins, Sam; DiMassimo, Betty; Rusnak, Pamela; Heuman, Douglas; Lalezari, Jacob; Sluder, Ann; Scorneaux, Bernard; Mosier, Sarah; Kowalczyk, Paul; Ribeill, Yves; Baugh, James; Gallay, Philippe
        Journal of Hepatology (2012), 57 (1), 47-54CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
        Background & Aims: SCY-635 is a non-immunosuppressive analog of cyclosporin A that inhibits cyclophilins A and B and hepatitis C virus (HCV) replication in vitro. In a phase 1b multi-dose escalation study, we evaluated the safety, plasma pharmacokinetics, and antiviral activity of 15 days of monotherapy with SCY-635 in adults with chronic genotype 1 HCV infection. Methods: Twenty adults with chronic HCV genotype 1 were randomized to SCY-635 oral doses of 100, 200, or 300 mg three times daily for 15 days. Results: No dose-limiting clin. or lab. toxicities were identified. On day 15, the mean decline in plasma viremia was 2.24 Â± 1.74 log10 IU/mL with SCY-635 900 mg/d.         Cyclosporin A inhibits hepatitis B and hepatitis D virus entry by cyclophilin-independent interference with the NTCP receptor
        Nkongolo, Shirin; Ni, Yi; Lempp, Florian A.; Kaufman, Christina; Lindner, Thomas; Esser-Nobis, Katharina; Lohmann, Volker; Mier, Walter; Mehrle, Stefan; Urban, Stephan
        Journal of Hepatology (2014), 60 (4), 723-731CODEN: JOHEEC; ISSN:0168-8278. (Elsevier B.V.)
        Chronic hepatitis B and hepatitis D are global health problems caused by the human hepatitis B and hepatitis D virus. The myristoylated preS1 domain of the large envelope protein mediates specific binding to hepatocytes by sodium taurocholate co-transporting polypeptide (NTCP). NTCP is a bile salt transporter known to be inhibited by cyclosporin A. This study aimed to characterize the effect of cyclosporin A on HBV/HDV infection. HepaRG cells, primary human hepatocytes, and susceptible NTCP-expressing hepatoma cell lines were applied for infection expts. The mode of action of cyclosporin A was studied by comparing the effect of different inhibitors, cyclophilin A/B/C-silenced cell lines as well as NTCP variants and mutants. Bile salt transporter and HBV receptor functions were investigated by taurocholate uptake and quantification of HBVpreS binding.Cyclosporin A inhibited hepatitis B and D virus infections during and - less pronounced - prior to virus inoculation. Binding of HBVpreS to NTCP was blocked by cyclosporin A concns. at 8 Î¼M. An NTCP variant deficient in HBVpreS binding but competent for bile salt transport showed resistance to cyclosporin A. Silencing of cyclophilins A/B/C did not abrogate transporter and receptor inhibition. In contrast, tacrolimus, a cyclophilin-independent calcineurin inhibitor, was inactive.HBV and HDV entry via sodium taurocholate co-transporting polypeptide is inhibited by cyclosporin A. The interaction between the drug and the viral receptor is direct and overlaps with a functional binding site of the preS1 domain, which mediates viral entry.

        From Chemical Tools to Clinical Medicines: Nonimmunosuppressive Cyclophilin Inhibitors Derived from the Cyclosporin and Sanglifehrin Scaffolds
        Sweeney, Zachary K.; Fu, Jiping; Wiedmann, Brigitte
        Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2014), 57 (17), 7145-7159CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
        A review. The cyclophilins are widely expressed enzymes that catalyze the interconversion of the cis and trans peptide bonds of prolines. The immunosuppressive natural products cyclosporine A and sanglifehrin A inhibit the enzymic activity of the cyclophilins. Chem. modification of both the cyclosporine and sanglifehrin scaffolds has produced many analogs that inhibit cyclophilins in vitro but have reduced immunosuppressive properties. Three nonimmunosuppressive cyclophilin inhibitors (alisporivir, SCY-635, and NIM811) have demonstrated clin. efficacy for the treatment of hepatitis C infection. Addnl. candidates are in various stages of preclin. development for the treatment of hepatitis C or myocardial reperfusion injury. Recent publications suggest that cyclophilin inhibitors may have utility for the treatment of diverse viral infections, inflammatory indications, and cancer. In this review, we document the structure-activity relationships of the nonimmunosuppressive cyclosporins and sanglifehrins in clin. and preclin. development. Aspects of the pharmacokinetic behavior and chem. biol. of these drug candidates are also described.

        Bioengineering and Semisynthesis of an Optimized Cyclophilin Inhibitor for Treatment of Chronic Viral Infection
        Hansson, Magnus Joakim; Moss, Steven James; Bobardt, Michael; Chatterji, Udayan; Coates, Nigel; Garcia-Rivera, Jose A.; Elmer, Eskil; Kendrew, Steve; Leyssen, Pieter; Neyts, Johan; Nur-E-Alam, Mohammad; Warneck, Tony; Wilkinson, Barrie; Gallay, Philippe; Gregory, Matthew Alan

        Inhibition of host-encoded targets, such as the cyclophilins, provides an opportunity to generate potent high barrier to resistance antivirals for the treatment of a broad range of viral diseases.         Cell permeability beyond the rule of 5
        Matsson, Paer; Doak, Bradley C.; Over, Bjoern; Kihlberg, Jan
        Advanced Drug Delivery Reviews (2016), 101 (), 42-61CODEN: ADDREP; ISSN:0169-409X. (Elsevier B.V.)
        Drug discovery for difficult targets that have large and flat binding sites is often better suited to compds. beyond the "rule of 5" (bRo5). However, such compds. carry higher pharmacokinetic risks, such as low soly. and permeability, and increased efflux and metab. Interestingly, recent drug approvals and studies suggest that cell permeable and orally bioavailable drugs can be discovered far into bRo5 space. Tactics such as redn. or shielding of polarity by N-methylation, bulky side chains and intramol. hydrogen bonds may be used to increase cell permeability in this space, but often results in decreased soly. Conformationally flexible compds. can, however, combine high permeability and soly., properties that are keys for cell permeability and intestinal absorption. Recent developments in computational conformational anal. will aid design of such compds. and hence prediction of cell permeability. Transporter mediated efflux occurs for most investigated drugs in bRo5 space, however it is commonly overcome by high local intestinal concns. on oral administration. In contrast, there is little data to support significant impact of transporter-mediated intestinal absorption in bRo5 space. Current knowledge of compd. properties that govern transporter effects of bRo5 drugs is limited and requires further fundamental and comprehensive studies.

        Conformational Flexibility, Internal Hydrogen Bonding, and Passive Membrane Permeability: Successful in Silico Prediction of the Relative Permeabilities of Cyclic Peptides
        Rezai, Taha; Bock, Jonathan E.; Zhou, Mai V.; Kalyanaraman, Chakrapani; Lokey, R. Scott; Jacobson, Matthew P.
        Journal of the American Chemical Society (2006), 128 (43), 14073-14080CODEN: JACSAT; ISSN:0002-7863. (American Chemical Society)
        We report an atomistic phys. model for the passive membrane permeability of cyclic peptides. The computational modeling was performed in advance of the expts. and did not involve the use of "training data". The model explicitly treats the conformational flexibility of the peptides by extensive conformational sampling in low (membrane) and high (water) dielec. environments. The passive membrane permeabilities of 11 cyclic peptides were obtained exptl. using a parallel artificial membrane permeability assay (PAMPA) and showed a linear correlation with the computational results with R2 = 0.96. In general, the results support the hypothesis, already well established in the literature, that the ability to form internal hydrogen bonds is crit. for passive membrane permeability and can be the distinguishing factor among closely related compds., such as those studied here. However, we have found that the no. of internal hydrogen bonds that can form in the membrane and the solvent-exposed polar surface area correlate more poorly with PAMPA permeability than our model, which quant. ests. the solvation free energy losses upon moving from high-dielec. water to the low-dielec. interior of a membrane.

        [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS],Â Google Scholar
        45
        The pregnane X receptor: from bench to bedside
        Ma, Xiaochao; Idle, Jeffrey R.; Gonzalez, Frank J.
        Expert Opinion on Drug Metabolism & Toxicology (2008), 4 (7), 895-908CODEN: EODMAP; ISSN:1742-5255. (Informa Healthcare)
        A review. The pregnane X receptor (PXR; NR1I2), a member of the nuclear receptor superfamily, regulates the expression of metabolic enzymes and transporters involved in the response of mammals to their chem. environment. To summarize the functions and clin. implications of PXR. In the current review, the clin. implications of PXR are discussed, and the use of genetically engineered PXR mouse models is highlighted. Recent advances in mouse models, including Pxr-null and PXR-humanized mice, provide in vivo tools for evaluating the physiol. functions of PXR and its role in controlling xenobiotic metab. and transport. By using the PXR knockout and humanized mouse models, PXR was found to influence drug-drug interactions, hepatic steatosis, and the homeostasis of vitamin D, bile acids, and steroid hormones. PXR was also shown to influence inflammatory bowel diseases.
